Sentences as attributes will also be treated under nominalized sentences below.

<h3 id="adverbials-nominal-wgs-and-verbs">Adverbials = Nominal WGs and verbs</h3>

Any language has the means of expressing the time, place, manner, and circumstances in which some verbal activity takes place. They may be subdivided semantically into those which give the setting of the whole situation; which have the whole sentence as their scope. Then there are the adverbials that are semantically connected with the verb, describing some aspect of the manner in which the action or event takes place. Sidaama has a number of resources for either of these two functions, and there is not a clear cut formal distinction between sentence adverbials and verb modifying ones.

The observant reader may have missed the category of Adverbs in the morphology section. The reason is that I claim that Sidaama does not have this category. Here follows a summary of Sidaama’s resources to express ‘adverbial’ meanings. As can be appreciated they are based on nouns and verbs.

The adverbial functions can be fulfilled by any nominal WG in the (unmarked) accusative case. Originally nouns, _gééʃʃa_ ‘quantity, size’ and _géde_ ‘likeness’ are frequent heads of such phrases: _lobó gééʃʃa_ ‘big quantity = very much’; _dánca géde_ ‘good likeness = very well’. These nouns are quite polysemous grammatically, in that they also have grammaticalized into the equivalents of subjunctions, among other functions as heads of temporal and final subordinate clauses respectively. Examples are given under ‘subordinate clauses’.

The so called postpositions are other examples of nouns in the ACC or OBL cases that function as adverbials, either alone or with a nominal WG as modifier. Ample examples are found under ‘case’ and ‘postpositions’. For some of these the grammaticalization process may have changed their prototypical meaning from nominal (noun) to relational (adposition), but the nominal sense remains in the word’s polysemy structure.

Oblique case inflection also serves to give different typical adverbial meanings to nouns, like instrumental, dative, ablative, temporal etc. For examples, see under ‘oblique cases’ above.

Further, nominalized sentences, being grammatically quite polysemous, in addition to the expected functions may also have temporal and conditional interpretations. These are treated below.

A great number of concepts that in English are typical adverbs, like fast, slowly, loudly, etc. are expressed by means of converbs or as progressive participles in Sidaama. These verbs are the heads of long or short sentences and give the opportunity to express any adverbial nuance of the main verb or superordinate sentence. For examples, see under ‘converbs’ and ‘progressive participle’ in the morphology section, and under ‘subordinate clauses’ below.

<h3 id="main-clauses">Main clauses</h3>

<h4 id="nominal-based-sentences">Nominal-based sentences</h4>

<h5 id="copulae--ho-and--te.">Copulae <em>-ho</em> and <em>-te</em>.</h5>

The simplest of sentences have the structure __It is N__ (identification), or __N=N__ (nominal equative sentences), or __V__ (just one finite verb, with the subject included in the tense/mode suffix).

Take any noun, add the COP suffix which corresponds to its class, and you have a sentence of the type: ‘It is an N’. This type of sentence contains only an overt PIV and a copula, while the subject will be understood from the situation or the context. The _-ho_ copula is used with the K-class, the _-te_ copula with the T-class. This construction corresponds to a predication where the predicative is a nominal entity, including adjectives and nominalized sentences.

(105) Examples of identificational sentences:

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i>harróóta-ho</i> ‘It is donkeys, they are donkeys’</p>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i>mánco-te</i> ‘It is a woman, she is a woman’</p>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i>mánco-ho<i> ‘It is a man, he is a man’</p>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i>ʔegennaasíne-te</i> ‘They are knowledgeable persons’</p>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i>mánna-ho</i> ‘They are people’</p>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i>mínna-te</i> ‘It is houses, they are houses’</p>

Any nominal word or WG in the nominative case can be added to specify the subject. The predicative may be a noun or an adjective or any nominalized item, even a nominalized sentence.

(106)

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i>ʔíse mánco-te</i> ‘She is a woman’</p>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i>ʔísi mánco-ho</i> ‘He is a man’</p>

If the predicative is an adjective, the class membership of the subject selects the form of the COP. If the PRED is a noun, this noun selects the copula, not the subject.

(107) Adjective as PRED:

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i>hákk-u dúll-i</o> (K) <i>dánca-<b>ho</b></i> ‘That stick is good’</p>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i>tín-i midáán-o</i> (T) <i>dánca-<b>te</b></i> ‘This cooking pot is good’</p>

Noun as PRED:

With overt marking it would have been \[…\]-\*_ha-ho_, and \[…\]-\*_ta-te_ respectively, but the _–\*ha/-\*ta_ is suppressed when the copula takes the form of a suffix, probably because it is phonologically similar to the copula. <h5 id="the-copula--vti">The copula <em>-</em>V<em>ti</em></h5>

This copula is used when the predicative head is modified, e.g. by an attribute or a possessive suffix. The V means that the vowel immediately preceding the _-ti_, (i.e. the accusative case ending, or the final vowel of any suffix) is lengthened.

(114)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>hákk</td>
<td>-u&nbsp;</td>
<td>mánc</td>
<td>-i&nbsp;</td>
<td>ʔánn</td>
<td>-a</td>
<td>-ʔya</td>
<td>-<strong>ati</strong></td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>that</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nom</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>man</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nom</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">m</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>father</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">poss</span>:my</td>
<td>-is</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘That man is my father.’</p>

(115)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>ʔíse&nbsp;</td>
<td>dánc</td>
<td>-a&nbsp;</td>
<td>mánco</td>
<td>-<strong>oti</strong></td>
<td></td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>she&nbsp;</td>
<td>good</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>woman</td>

<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">cop</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘She is a good woman.’</p>

The COP -V*ti* is also used with certain pronominal PREDs, like interrogative pronouns _ma_ ‘what’, _ʔae_ ‘who’, and others. Note the answer to ‘What is your name?’ (118).

(116)

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i>hákku má-<b>áti</b></i>? ‘What is that?’</p>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i>ʔíse ʔaé-éti</i>? ‘Who is she?’</p>

Omitting the subjects in (116) would be possible; the questions then would mean ‘what is it?’ and ‘who is it?’ respectively, referring to some unknown entity in the context. Changing the COP, however, would give different meaning of the questions altogether:

(117)

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i>mááho</i>? ‘What is it? = What is the matter?’</p>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i>ʔaéte</i>?‘Whose is it?’ (referring to a T-class noun)</p>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i>ʔaého</i>? ‘Whose is it?’ (referring to a K-class noun)</p>

Please note: ‘_mááho?_’ is not to be confused with ‘_mááho!_’ which means ‘OK, I agree, I’ll do it’. It has nothing to do with the COP _-ho_; the word is abbreviated from _mááhoyye_ ‘OK, etc.’

(118)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>súʔm</td>
<td>-i</td>
<td>-kki&nbsp;</td>
<td>ʔa</td>
<td>-é</td>
<td>-étíʔ&nbsp;</td>
<td>súʔm</td>
<td>-i</td>
<td>-ʔya&nbsp;</td>
<td>baɗáás</td>
<td>-a</td>
<td>-ati</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>name</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nom</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">m</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">poss</span>:your.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">sg</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>who</td>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘What is your name? My name is Badaasa.’</p>

<h5 id="the-copula-ʔikk--to-be">The copula <em>ʔikk-</em> ‘to be’</h5>

The suffixed copulae mentioned above cannot occur on other levels than the main finite sentence. In any kind of subordinate clauses as well as in imperative/jussive they are replaced by the full verb copula _ʔikk-_ ‘to be’, which is inflected in all ways like other full verbs. It has to be noted that with the future/inchoative meaning of ‘become’ or with the meaning ‘behave’ this verb is not a replacement of the COP, but a full verb with the mentioned lexical meanings. An example of this usage is given first.

(119)

<td>-ó</td>
<td>-te&nbsp;</td>
<td>géde&nbsp;</td>
<td>ʔikk</td>
<td>-éémmo’&nbsp;</td>
<td>yé</td>
<td>-é</td>
</tr>

<h5 id="negation-of-nominal-based-identificational-and-equative-sentences">Negation of nominal-based identificational and equative sentences</h5>

The proclitic negative particle _dí-_ will negate any sentence with copula _-ho, -te,_ or -V*ti* by being prefixed to the PRED. It will attract the H/Stress from wherever it would be in the positive PRED. A predication involving the _ʔikk-_ copula is negated by the same prefix, but like with all other full verbs the _dí-_ is attached to the verb, not to the PRED as with the suffixed copulae.

Examples:

(122)

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i><b>dí</b>=ʔegennaasine-te</i> ‘They are not knowledgeable persons’</p>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i><b>dí</b>=minna-te</i> ‘They are not houses’</p>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i>ʔínsa <b>dí</b>=manna-ho</i> ‘They are not people’</p>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px"><i>nínke <b>dí</b>=ʔegennaasine-te</i> ‘We are not knowledgeable persons’</p>

However, if the copula is -V*ti*, there is fluctuation between the prefixing of _dí-_, which is then proclitic to the PRED, even if it is a whole WG – on one hand – and using the suppletive verb _ʔikk-_ ‘to be’ in its negated form on the other hand:

(123)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td><strong>dí=</strong></td>
<td>ʔann</td>
<td>-a</td>
<td>-ʔya</td>
<td>-ati&nbsp;</td>
<td>or:&nbsp;</td>
<td>ʔánn</td>
<td>-a</td>
<td>-ʔya&nbsp;</td>
<td><strong>dí=</strong></td>
<td>ʔikk</td>
<td>-ino</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td><span style="font-variant: small-caps">neg</span></td>
<td>-father</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">poss</span>:my</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">cop</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>father</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span></td>

As can be observed from (125), _dí-_ is a proclitic at the WG level, not the word level.

<h4 id="verb-based-sentences">Verb-based sentences</h4>

<h5 id="concord-between-the-verb-and-the-head-of-the-nominal-wg-as-subject">Concord between the verb and the head of the nominal WG as subject</h5>

Generally the K-class nouns call for the K-labelled 3<sup>rd</sup> person and the masculine 1<sup>st</sup> and 2<sup>nd</sup> person forms in the verbal paradigms, and T-class nouns call for the T-labelled 3<sup>rd</sup> person and the feminine 1<sup>st</sup> and 2<sup>nd</sup> person forms in the verbal paradigms. The 1PL and 2PL agree with the pronouns _nínke_ ‘we’ and _kíʔne_ ‘you<span>.</span>PL’ respectively. _ʔínsa_ ‘they’ agrees with the 3T. It is therefore superfluous to have a line for 3PL in the verb paradigms. Ample examples of this agreement are found in the morphology section, here are some additional ones.

(126)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>baɗáás</td>
<td>-i(<strong>K-</strong>class)&nbsp;</td>
<td>lekk</td>
<td>-á&nbsp;</td>
<td>hiik’k’</td>
<td>-am</td>
<td>-í</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>Badaasa</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nom</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>leg</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>break</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pas</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pfct</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3k</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘Badaasa was broken with respect to a leg = broke a leg.’</p>

<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">poss</span>:my</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">obl</span>(to)&nbsp;</td>
<td>come</td>
<td>-CVB1&nbsp;</td>

<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>descend</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span>1</td>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘<b>I (fem)</b> come to my mother and (afterwards) <b>I (fem)</b> fetch water.’</p>

In general, the lexical class of the nucleus noun in the Subject WG is the determinant of the verb form with respect to agreement, even when the K-class does not refer to an entity with masculine natural gender, and the T-class does not refer to an entity with female natural gender. In (129) the subject is semantically plural and refers to natural females. Belonging to the K-class however, the agreeing form of the verb reflects neither feminine nor plural, but is 3K following strictly the class of the subject noun.

(129)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>séénn</td>

<h5 id="the-three-vv-derivations--am--iɗ-and--is">The three V→V derivations, <em>-am</em>, <em>-iɗ</em>, and <em>-is</em></h5>

Out of the V→V derivations in the verb the valency of the base (active) verb is changed in the Passive (_-am_) which has no overt agent, and Causative (_-is_ in all its varieties) which add participants along the ‘flow of energy’. The active verb is transitive or intransitive. Only transitive verbs are passivized with the _-am_ suffix, while there is no such restriction on the ‘Unspecified subject paradigm’ (US). The Autobenefactive (_-iɗ_ in all its varieties) has the same valency as the base verb. Below, the PAS and the US are put next to each other, because of their similarity in not expressing the agent. Sufficient examples of the Causative and the Autobenefactive are found in the Morphology section.

<h5 id="passive">Passive</h5>

The Passive works more or less like in English; the subject specifies the patient, and the agent is not expressed.

(133)

<h5 id="the-paradigm-of-the-unspecified-subject-us">The paradigm of the unspecified subject (US)</h5>

The US paradigm is basically active, but the sentence has no overt subject. The patient is included in the accusative case, i.e. as DO. In (137) the word _ʔass-ina**nní**-re_ means ‘that which one does (in general)’, or, according to the possibilities of the IMPF and the actual situation, ‘that which one has to do (in the future)’. The _-re_ suffix signals both that the word is nominalized, is direct object, and it implies more actions than one.

In the following example there are seemingly two objects, where one (the second) consists of a sentence which is not marked in any way. This is often the case with objects of ‘to say’ which are direct quotes. Here the direct quote is the nominal identificational sentence _ʃáʃa-ho_ ‘he is a coward’. Otherwise sentences as DO are nominalized, which is also the case with the first object, delimited by the outer square brackets. The superordinate sentence consists of the verb ‘one does not say (US)’ plus the mentioned DOs. The structure corresponds to the English structure with ‘call’, e.g. ‘_He called him a coward’._ The example is a proverb.

(138)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">

<td>-inó]</td>
<td>-ha]&nbsp;</td>

</tr>

<td>-<strong><span style="font-variant: small-caps">cvb</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">us</span></strong>&nbsp;</td>
<td>spend.day</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">cvb</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span>.<strong><span style="font-variant: small-caps">us</span></strong></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘The boy one quarrelled with yesterday, one stays with today after being reconciled.’ (-me &gt; -ne, progressive assimilation of the /m/ in -am.)</p>

This paradigm has sometimes been called ‘impersonal passive’. And only formal, not semantic, characteristics are lost by translating it into English as Passive: (138) ‘The one who throws whatever he has in hand __is not called__ a coward’ (139): ‘The __trusted__ liver may choke you’. With intransitive verbs however, that is not possible (140): \*’it is stayed with’. So, one can describe the US as a way of passivizing intransitive verbs.

<h3 id="subordinate-clauses">Subordinate clauses</h3>

In the previous sections there have already been a lot of examples with subordinate clauses, which the reader surely has been able to identify. In the following, the clauses are classified semantically, more or less according to their English equivalents. But because the nominalized sentences display an interesting polysemy structure, they are given their own section below, which also overlaps with previous sections, for example in the treatment of temporal clauses.

<h4 id="conditional-clauses">Conditional clauses</h4>

These are formed with _-ro_ ‘if’ suffixed to the PFV, including the PFV of the US conjugation, which ends in _-ni_. The meaning of this construction is the ‘normal condition’: if the condition is fulfilled, the action will take place, if not, the action will not take place. The main verb may be in IMPF, PFV, or IMP.

(141)

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘If one understands all that one looks for, one finds other things as well.’</p>

<h4 id="hypothetical-conditional-clauses">Hypothetical conditional clauses</h4>

When the condition is not fulfilled, the conditional clause has the verb in the ‘conditional hypothetical’ (HYP) conjugation. The main clause is in the imperfective tense, sometimes with the hypothetical verb _wolet-_ ‘be otherwise’, or with the suffixes _-nka-lla._ When these two suffixes are not together, the first one is a nominalizer/definiteness marker, and the second one means something like ‘fortunately’, e.g. in greetings. It is difficult to pinpoint their contribution to the meaning of the conditional sentence below, so I try with EMPH, emphasizers.

The functional possibilities of the _-ta_ NR suffix will be treated below.

<h4 id="concessive-clauses">Concessive clauses</h4>

These are constructed the same way as conditional clauses, but the suffix _-no_ ‘also, too’ is added after _-ro_.

(146)

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘‘<b>Even though</b> I may be shorter, I am still your father’, said the donkey to the mule.’ (Proverb)</p>

The Sidaama equivalent of ‘even so’, ‘all the same’, is the copula in the concessive form:_ʔikk-í-ró-no ‘_even though it might be (like that)…’

<h4 id="temporal-clauses">Temporal clauses</h4>

Here will be treated those temporal clauses that are not based on and formally like nominalized clauses; those are treated below.

Bear in mind that nouns in the accusative case may function as adverbials of any kind. Several kinds of adverbial clauses are construed as a head noun preceded by a relative clause which modifies that noun. Among the nouns used for temporal clauses we frequently find _ʔalba_ ‘face’, _gedensa_ ‘afterness’, _woite_ (_woote_) ‘time’, _yanna_ ‘time’, and _waro_ ‘time’. The two former ones are normally inflected is some oblique case, while the latter three are normally found in the accusative.

<h4 id="before">Before</h4>

One possibility is to express this notion with _ʔálba_ ‘face’ and the case suffix -V*nni*. The verb in the infinitive is an attribute to _ʔalba_: ‘at the face of (the fact) that he went, in front of his going’.

__‘Before__ one sees the donkey coming, one has heard its whining.’

There is a semantic connection between the notion of ‘before doing something’ and ‘without doing something’; the latter is implied in the former. The construction in (121) can also be translated ‘without seeing the donkey coming’, which is also obviously true until one suddenly sees it. The following proverb illustrates this further:

<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">obl</span>(at)&nbsp;</td>
<td>die</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pst</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3t</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘Lending out <b>without</b> having enough (for herself), she died <b>without</b> getting paid back / <b>before</b> she could be paid back.’</p>

<h4 id="while">While</h4>

The notion of actions simultaneous with the main verb can be expressed by the nouns _yanna_ or _woite_ (_wote_), both meaning ‘time’, preceded by an attributive clause. The following ironic proverb serves as an example. Note that the case ending _-nni_ is not necessary, the plain accusative would mean the same. Note how Sidaama allows you to identify the participants in this drama as two men (talking to each other) and a woman (the patient) by the verb suffixes.

(151)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>‘re</td>
<td>-itinó’&nbsp;</td>
<td>y</td>
<td>-éémmo&nbsp;</td>
<td><strong>woit</strong></td>
<td><strong>-é</strong></td>
<td>-nni&nbsp;</td>
<td>‘wóyy</td>
<td>-a&nbsp;</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>die</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pst</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3t</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>say</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf.1m</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td><strong>time</strong></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">obl</span>(at)&nbsp;</td>
<td>better</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nom</span>&nbsp;</td>

<td>gal</td>
<td>-tú?’&nbsp;</td>
<td>yá</td>
<td>-atto</td>
<td>-ʔe</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>spend.night</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pfct</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3t</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>say</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span>.2<span style="font-variant: small-caps">m</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">io</span>:me</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘When I say (Lit: The time that I say) ‘She has died’, how come you ask me ‘Did she feel better last night?’’</p>

Far more frequently, however, simultaneity is expressed by means of the progressive participle suffixed with _-nni_. Several examples are found under ‘the progressive participle’ above. As noted there, _-nna_ replaces _-nni_ when there is a change of subject. Although a few examples have been attested where both suffixes occur on the same participle, I suggest that replacement is the general pattern for reasons of euphony (same as the reason for omitting one of two similar suffixes, e.g. _-\*ha -ho &gt; -ho_, but that the construction originally was to have both, and that this still is viable in this case. See the following two examples.

Both the preceding examples are from a story about coffee, and the reader may appreciate how important this drink is in the culture.

The converb, normally enumerating successive events and causal chains, may also enumerate simultaneous events.

<h4 id="after">After</h4>

In normal conversation and narrative, if there is no particular focus on the fact that something occurs after something else, the converb conjugation is used to imply temporal succession of events.

However, Sidaama also expresses this notion with the noun _gedénsa_ ‘afterness’ suffixed with -V*nni*:

(155)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>[mimmit<a href="#ref28" id="28" data-toggle="tooltip" title="mito ‘one’ with reduplication: mim-mito."><sup>28</sup></a></td>
<td>-ó&nbsp;</td>
<td>keer</td>
<td>-é&nbsp;</td>
<td>haaɗ</td>
<td>-í]</td>
<td>-<strong>hu</strong>&nbsp;</td>
<td>gedens</td>
<td>-á</td>
<td>-anni</td>
<td>…</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>each.other</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>peace</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>receive</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pfct</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3k</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nmr</span>.<strong><span style="font-variant: small-caps">gen</span></strong>&nbsp;</td>
<td>afterness</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">obl</span>(at)</td>
<td></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘After they had exchanged greetings (lit.: peace) with each other …’</p>

It is important to note that the whole of (127) is a case inflected nominal WG that includes an attribute. The head of the case inflected nominal is _gedénsa_, and its attribute is a sentence (delimited by square brackets), comparable to a relative clause. Then, of course, the whole construction is transformed to an adverbial by the _-nni_ case ending. The difference between the bracketed attribute and a relative clause is that there is a genitive relation between modifier and the head, in a way exactly parallel to (128).

(156)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>ros</td>
<td>-<strong>ú</strong>&nbsp;</td>
<td>gedens</td>
<td>-á</td>
<td>-anni</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>lesson</td>
<td>-<strong><span style="font-variant: small-caps">gen</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">u</span></strong>&nbsp;</td>
<td>afterness</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">obl</span>(at)</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘After the lesson (lit. at the afterness <b>of</b> the lesson)’</p>

So a paraphrase that reveals the structure of (127) would be ‘At the afterness of the fact that they exchanged greetings’. To allow the sentence to be in the genitive case, it has to be nominalized. _-hu_, as shown elsewhere, is a nominalizer and at the same time a case marker for the nominative and, as here, the genitive. This way of construing various subordinate clauses is extremely common in Sidaama, and observed above with _ʔalba_ and _woite._ The nouns frequently used for heads in such nominal WGs, stand a good chance of being grammaticalized into subordinate connectors, subjunctions. And that probably has happened with this noun and other nouns to a varying degree. Compare the similar process seen in the ‘postpositions’ above.

I think the grammaticalization process manifests itself in the fact that the nominalizer/case indicator is optional, like in (129). If it is omitted, there is no compelling reason to say it is in the genitive, but it still has to be understood as an attribute because of its position before a noun.

(157)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>[seejj</td>
<td>-ó&nbsp;</td>
<td>ʔuu</td>
<td>-itú](</td>
<td>-hu)&nbsp;</td>
<td>gedensaanni&nbsp;</td>
<td>…</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>advice</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>give</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pfct</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3t</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>(*<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nmr</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">gen</span>)&nbsp;</td>
<td>etc.</td>
<td></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘After they had given the advice…’</p>

The converb form of _kaʔ-_ ‘to rise’ is also on a grammaticalization path towards a subjunction meaning ‘after’. In this function it is optionally followed by _-nti-nni_.

<h4 id="the-converb-conjugation-cvb-1">The converb conjugation (CVB)</h4>

In principle there is no limit to the number of possible converbs in a sentence; up to twelve have been observed with one main verb. This feature is a characteristic trait of Sidaama, and very few sentences are without it in normal speech or in narratives. The meanings may be temporal sequence, simoultaneous events, cause, or simply a listing of unrelated activities. For examples, see immediately above and under ‘the converb conjugation’ in the morphology section. Ample examples are also found everywhere in this grammar, as the phenomenon is so pervasive. Here just one sentence is added.

<h4 id="causal-clauses">Causal clauses</h4>

The notion of ‘therefore’, as the introduction to independent sentences, is expressed by _kunníra_, which is a demonstrative pronoun plus _-ra_, OBL(for), or _korkáátuno_ ‘and the reason is that…’ In a similar manner any sentence nominalized by _-hu_ and with the oblique case suffix _-ra_, combined into _-nnira,_ expresses the cause of another event. This will be treated under ‘Nominalized sentences’ below.

The noun _daafo_ ‘cause’, with or without the _-ra_ suffix, with an attributive clause, will also function as a subordinate causal clause.

(159)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>

<td>gara&nbsp;</td>
<td>ʔegenn</td>
<td>-tió<a href="#ref30" id="30" data-toggle="tooltip" title="-tió < -tinó"><sup>30</sup></a>&nbsp;</td>
<td>mánn</td>
<td>-i&nbsp;</td>
<td>nó]&nbsp;</td>

<td>-rá&nbsp;</td>
<td>…</td>
</tr>

<td></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘because there are people who know the way in which one works = how to work…’</p>

The expression ‘_nó __daaf-i-rá___’ may be replaced by ‘_nóó**hura**_’.

<h4 id="finalconsecutive-clauses">Final/consecutive clauses</h4>

Purpose is expressed by means of the person inflected infinitive with _-ra_, with infinitive plus _-te_, or with the noun _gede_ ‘likeness, purpose’ preceded by an attributive clause. The suffix _-ra_ expresses the purpose of the subject of the main verb, so that the subject of the PINF will be co-referent with it. _gede_, on the other hand, expresses ‘induced purpose’, thus it will often be used with the contents of an order, a wish etc. for someone else to do. This use of _gede_ corresponds to the use of the Amharic _ïndä(mm)-_, which can depend of borrowing in either direction between the two languages. The construction INF+_te_, like _tír-a-te_ ‘in order to solve’ also corresponds to the Amharic construction _lä_+INF, like _lä-mäftat_ ‘in order to solve’, and the frequency of this construction in translated texts indicates that is probably a translation loan from Amharic. So probably the first mentioned way is the oldest genuine Sidaama way of expressing purpose, while the two latter ones are more innovative, and influenced by Amharic.

(160)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>rós</td>

<td>k’arr</td>
<td>-á&nbsp;</td>
<td><strong>tír</strong></td>
<td><strong>-a</strong></td>
<td><strong>-te</strong>&nbsp;</td>
<td>bud</td>
<td>-é&nbsp;</td>

<h4 id="comparative-clauses">Comparative clauses</h4>

Likeness is expressed with the noun _gede_ described above, preceded by an attributive clause.

<h3 id="nominalized-clauses">Nominalized clauses</h3>

By definition, whatever is nominalized may function as a constituent in a sentence, but the semantics of the different nominalized clauses may differ from each other.

<h4 id="nominalized-sentences-as-referring-expressions">Nominalized sentences as referring expressions</h4>

One of the most useful functions of language is that it enables the speakers to pick out an entity in the world, name it, and attach a comment of some kind to it. This section shows the possibilities of Sidaama to use sentences in the function of referring to entities.

(166)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">

<td>té&nbsp;</td>
<td>bága&nbsp;</td>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘<b>You who have not competed</b>, how did you ascend that slope?’ (REC = reciprocal, ‘with anyone’)</p>

The nominalized bracketed sentence refers to the addressee in the conversation, marked as SUBJ and K-class, the latter in this case corresponding to natural masculine gender. The person referred to is only signalled in three portmanteau verb suffixes which also inform about tense/aspect and finiteness. But the _-hu_ suffix tells that the reference of the bracketed sentence is not the verbal action, but the person responsible for it.

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘Identifying that which demonstrates good and bad behaviour, tell (it) to your teacher.’</p>

Here the DO is a sentence marked with _-ha_. This is marked as K-class for no apparent reason. It cannot be with reference to the last previously mentioned noun in the context, because that is _misile_ ‘picture’, which is T-class. The children had been asked to look at different pictures to identify what was good and bad behaviour.

</table>

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>ful</td>
<td>-ítta</td>
<td>-<strong>ti</strong>]&nbsp;</td>
<td>ʔaj</td>
<td>-jée</td>
<td>-<strong>nná</strong>…&nbsp;</td>
<td>?</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>exit</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pfct</span>.2F</td>
<td>-<strong><span style="font-variant: small-caps">nmr</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">t</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nom</span></strong>&nbsp;</td>
<td>be.little</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">cvb</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3t</span></td>
<td>-<strong><span style="font-variant: small-caps">ns</span>…</strong></td>
<td></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘Is it too little that you went into and out of my mouth on a bad day…?’</p>

The SUBJ sentence is marked with the T-class nominalizer, and its verb shows concord with it in class (T-class nominalization agrees with the 3SG.F (T) of the verb). Now the converb conjugation has the same forms for 2SG as for 3SG.F (T); the verb suffix before sandhi rules made it opaque was _-te(e_). Could the SUBJ sentence not refer to the addressee, like in (8)? The translation would have been: ‘__You who entered into my mouth on a bad day__, are you too small?’ This would in fact have been possible were it not for the suffix _-nna_, which signals ‘new subject follows’ with the converbs. And the SUBJ in the following part of the sentence (not included here) is 2F, precluding \*_you (f)_ from being the SUBJ of _be too small_. This phenomenon is also illustrated inside the SUBJ sentence, where the same suffix signals that ‘day’ henceforth is relieved from its SUBJ duties (for the verb ‘be bad’) and another SUBJ takes over, viz. ‘you’, SUBJ for ‘enter’. In order for the example to make sense, it should be added that it is from a story where a sacred ibis put its beak into the mouth of a lion to relieve it of a piece of meat that was stuck in its throat.

<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">gen</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">u</span>&nbsp;</td>

<td>each.other.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>resemble</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3k</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>shape&nbsp;</td>
<td>see</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">ab</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pst</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3t</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>-<strong>re</strong>]&nbsp;</td>
<td>mítto</td>
<td>-ba&nbsp;</td>
<td>k’ol</td>
<td>-té&nbsp;</td>
<td>borrééss</td>
<td>-i!</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nmr</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pl</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>one</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">obl</span>(at)&nbsp;</td>
<td>turn</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">cvb</span>.2<span style="font-variant: small-caps">sg</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>write</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">imp</span>.2<span style="font-variant: small-caps">sg</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘…from among these characters, write in one place <b>those which have shapes that resemble each other</b>!’</p>

In this sentence, it might be argued that the bracketed nominalized sentence functions attributively, describing the _fidalla_ ‘letters’, previously mentioned. If this were the case, it would not harm my overall argumentation, my aim being to show the formal unity of referring and describing sentences. Still I think those two functions can be kept apart and identified in most cases. And the structural argument here is that _gíddo_ ‘inside’ is the superordinate entity in the phrase _fidallá-te gíddo_ ‘inside/from among the letters’, and even if an attributive sentence may follow its head, it must be adjacent to its head. So the best option is to interpret the bracketed sentence in (143) as having a referring function.

<h4 id="sentences-as-attributes-adjectival-descriptive-sentences">Sentences as attributes (adjectival, descriptive sentences)</h4>

Sidaama grammar distinguishes between WGs which have one attribute and WGs which have more attributes, in that last preceding attributes are marked differently from non-last preceding attributes. Attributes which follow their head, are marked as non-last preceding attributes, unless they are adjectives, for which case see below. With this exception, the marking is present regardless of the grammatical status or complexity of the attributes. Here are first given some examples with adjectives and K-class nouns, the combination which shows the pattern the best. In these examples the abbreviations stand for unmodified attribute, modified head, non-last and last preceding attribute; apart from that, the morphological tagging is simplified:

(172)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>dánc</td>
<td>-<strong>u</strong>&nbsp;</td>
<td>mánn</td>
<td>-i&nbsp;</td>
<td>nó</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>good</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">u</span>.P<span style="font-variant: small-caps">recp</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">attr</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>people</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">mod</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">h</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nom</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>are.present</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

The _-ha_ suffix signals that it belongs with the head noun of the WG, _ʔuduunnicco_, as a non-last attribute, while the -_u_ signals that it is an unmodified last preceding

attribute. If an adjective as attribute follows its head noun, which frequently occurs with the sub-category quantifiers, the noun is marked as unmodified, and the adjective as last attribute, also unmodified:

Nested in a way quite typical for Sidaama, the following example illustrates a complex attribute located after its head:

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘Turn the first pages in the book of instruction of the language of Sidaama!’ (The first ACC is an example of the ADV function, indicating place)</p>

The contents of the boldfaced brackets might have preceded the head, but in that case the _-ha_ would have been superfluous. It seems to be a pattern that attributes in a position which could cause difficulty of interpretation because of distance from their head, or following instead of preceding their head, are marked in a way which shows what noun they qualify. The mark is a nominalizer rather than a case ending, it agrees with its head noun in class, case, and number, thus easing the burden of the interpreter. We will see from what follows, that nominalized sentences as attributes follow the same basic pattern.

If a sentence functions as the sole attribute, i.e. corresponds to a sole relative clause, it is not marked, but simply precedes the nominal head.

(176)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>[[diinaggé</td>
<td>-ra&nbsp;</td>
<td>so</td>
<td>-inó]&nbsp;</td>
<td>sókka]&nbsp;</td>
<td>tógo&nbsp;</td>
<td>y</td>
<td>-itannó</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>Diinagge</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">obl</span>(to)&nbsp;</td>
<td>send</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pst</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3k</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>letter&nbsp;</td>
<td>like.this&nbsp;</td>
<td>say</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3t</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘The letter <b>which he sent to Diinagge</b>, says like this.’</p>

Much less frequently, but still with ample text examples, attributive clauses may follow their head. In that case they are obligatorily marked, and the suffixes they are marked with, are identical to the nominalizing suffixes, and in this work taken to be the same. There is no overt marking of relative clauses that is different from the affixes that nominalize. The main signal that indicates that the clause is relative is that it is a complete sentence that precedes a noun. In (149) the inner bracket contains such a relative clause following its head, and therefore marked as nominal.

(177)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">

<td>tan</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pas</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3k</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>way&nbsp;</td>
<td>tell</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3t</span>s.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span></td>
<td>-if</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘…if the text <b>which is prepared and presented</b> tells about the method with which skin is tanned…’</p>

Obviously nominal*izer* is not a good name for the marker of a describing or attributive clause, so one has to bear in mind that it is the almost arbitrary name of a suffix whose functions include nominalization among others. Because it seems to be the prototypical nouns that have the most flexible variety of functions, rather than the adjectives, it may, however, be well advised to label these suffixes nominalizers, and then describe the constructions they occur in as nominal, attributive, adverbial, or predicative as the case may be, just as one can do with nouns. When sentences function attributively, the class membership/number of the head noun governs the gender/number inflection of the verb, and the corresponding nominalizer. Thus _-ti_ in this example is the marker for a T-class noun, viz. _nibaabe_ ‘text’, and it is in the NOM case being part of the WG which is SUBJ for _kultannó_ ‘it tells’, and thus in full agreement with this verb.

(178)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>[k’ára&nbsp;</td>
<td>ʔaf</td>
<td>-iɗ</td>
<td>-ɗinó</td>
<td>-<strong>ti]</strong>&nbsp;</td>
<td>baattó</td>
<td>-te&nbsp;</td>
<td>ʔum</td>
<td>-ánco</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>Sharpness&nbsp;</td>
<td>see</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">ab</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pst</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3t</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nmr</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">t</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nom</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">non</span>last.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">attr</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>soil</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">gen</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">u</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>dig</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">ag</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘A digging tool which has sharpness, for soil (=a plough)’</p>

These examples have shown that the inventory of devices for creating relative clauses is the same as those which nominalize. We have also seen, _en passant_, that the same devices also nominalize other units, like case inflected nouns, when they are needed as attributes (e.g. (147) _roosíha_ and below (151) _ʔafúúbbanníti_). When the attributive clause is in the most frequent position for modifiers, viz. immediately preceding its head, it normally has no marker. Attributive clauses in other positions, viz. pre-ultimate or following their head, are marked with the available nominalizers in agreement with that head.

<h4 id="sentences-as-predications-as-prd">Sentences as predications (=as PRD)</h4>

As do nouns and adjectives, complete nominalized sentences also function as PRD. In that construction they always get suffixed some form of a copula, either _-ho, -te_, -V*ti* or the equivalent conjugated copula verb _ʔikk-_. In the same way as when nouns and adjectives are used for predication, it can be argued that sentences in that function either refer or describe, as will be made clear from the following examples. Only one example with a T-class SUBJ head is given, but the same pattern appears for K-class and plural SUBJ’s. In those cases the nominalizer is _-ha_ and _-re_ respectively, as expected from the table (7).

(179)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>ʔaf</td>
<td>-úúbba</td>
<td>-nní</td>
<td>-ti&nbsp;</td>
<td>ʔaf</td>
<td>-íí&nbsp;</td>
<td>jirte&nbsp;</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>languge</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pl</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">obl</span>(in)</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nmr</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">t</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nom</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">non</span>last.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">attr</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>language</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">gen</span>&nbsp;</td>

</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">

<td><strong>-t’</strong></td>
<td><strong>-itinó</strong></td>
<td><strong>-ta]&nbsp;</strong></td>
<td><strong>ʔikk</strong></td>
<td>-á</td>
<td>-se&nbsp;</td>
<td>hubat</td>
<td>-néémmo</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>differ.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">recp</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">ab</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pst</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3t</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nmr</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">t</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>be</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">inf</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">poss</span>:her&nbsp;</td>
<td>remember</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span>.1<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pl</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘We remember that the grammars in languages are different (or: entities which differ) from each other.’</p>

According to what we have seen above, it is not easy do decide whether the bracketed PRD refers to __entities__ which are different from each other, and equals the grammars of languages with these entities, or stands for the __quality__ of being different from each other, and so states that grammars are like that. What we can say is that the suffix _-ta_ nominalizes, and that the resulting composite nominal units have the same capacity as lexical nominal units to refer _and_ to describe. On the other hand, there is full agreement in class and number between the SUBJ of the copula and the nominalizing suffix of the PRD. This might be taken as an argument that the PRD sentence is adjectival, according to the test for prototypical adjective-_hood_ in (2); if the PRD does not influence the choice of copula, it is an adjective.

Sentences as PRD with the uninflectable copula have no overt nominalizer, as in (152):

(180)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>tíni&nbsp;</td>
<td>godóʔle&nbsp;</td>
<td>[meʔé&nbsp;</td>
<td>ʔikk</td>
<td>-iné&nbsp;</td>
<td>godoʔl</td>
<td>-inanní]</td>
<td>-(*ta)</td>
<td>-té?</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>This&nbsp;</td>
<td>game&nbsp;</td>
<td>how.many&nbsp;</td>
<td>be</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">us</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">cvb</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>play</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">uspsubj</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">cop</span></td>
<td></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘This game, how many players does it have?’ (Lit.: This game is (something) which how many being does one play (it)?)</p>

If the sentence structure had called for an inflected copula, the PRD in the bracket would have been extended with _-ta_. The PRD having no overt nominalizer, the agreement with the SUBJ appears only in the COP.

<td></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘It is also (a thing which is) convenient for processing in strong machinery.’</p>

The sentence has no overt SUBJ, but the previous context deals with _k’ac’c’a_ (K), ‘the sisal palm’, from which rope is made. That topic governs the choice of copula and calls for the ‘masculine’ singular in the verb which is nominalized.

One reason why the nominalizer is omitted in these cases could be the reluctance to repeat a phonologically similar morph, known as the ‘repeated morph constraint’ (Menn &amp; MacWhinney 1984). The /t/ and /h/ (&gt;\*/k/) of the two copula may be historically connected to the /t/ and /h/ of the nominalizers, so that _godoʔl-inanni-\*ta-te_ and _ʔinj-aanno-\*ha-ho_ of (152) and (153) are avoided. In support of this possibility it can be shown that nominalizer and COP can coexist when they are phonologically more different, and clearly not related:

(182)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>Hiyy</td>
<td>-ééyye&nbsp;</td>
<td>[máál</td>
<td>-a&nbsp;</td>
<td>ʔit</td>
<td>-tannó</td>
<td>-<strong>re</strong>]</td>
<td>-eti</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>wolf</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pl</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nom</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>meat</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>eat</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3t</span></td>
<td>-<strong><span style="font-variant: small-caps">nmr</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pl</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span></strong></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">cop</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘Wolves are (entities) which eat meat.’</p>

The SUBJ is inflected for plural, and governs the choice of plural verb form and plural nominalizer. As can be seen, the chosen copula coexists with the nominalizer, which is phonological different, and also unrelated phonologically. It must also be noted that the COP _-ho_ and _-te_ never occur with a PRD sentence nominalized with _-re_, which may be because the two are more strictly tied up with the nominal classes, so as to agree with the K-class and T-class respectively, while -V*ti* is insensitive to nominal class and its presence in a sentence depends on other factors (modified or unmodified head).

In this section we have seen that nominalized sentences function as PRDs, and that they can occur with or without the overt NR markers, according to patterns which have to do with the copula. Sentences with this function, with or without markers, can be interpreted as referring or describing, like other nominals.

One might ask the following fundamental questions: Why bother to express something as N1=N2, when N2 is based on a verb anyway? Why not simply use the finite verb as a predication, when that is what we have verbs for after all? Why not simply ‘Wolves eat meat’ instead of the more cumbersome (154)? The answer is not straightforward, but may perhaps be sought along the same lines as is found to be the motivations for the cleft sentences (treated below). It is felt somehow that a nominalization communicates the intended message better, or is more efficient, than the plain verb. In cleft sentences you nominalize a process in order to attach a focused comment to it; here you pick out a nominal entity and construe the processual comment as a nominal entity too.

<h4 id="cleft-sentences-cs">Cleft sentences (CS)</h4>

This phenomenon represents a way of structuring the information such that the known or topic information is given in the form of a nominalized sentence functioning as the SUBJ, and the new information or comment, or in questions the unknown entity, is given the form of a PRED plus COP.

The SUBJ refers either to an implied or explicit participant in the nominalized sentence, or to its entire semantic contents construed as a thing. According to this division, I classify the CSs into three types according to the reference of the SUBJ.

(183)

CS-1: The reference of the SUBJ is the __SUBJ__ of the nominalized verb. The PRED elaborates it (makes it explicit).

CS-2: The reference of the SUBJ is the __DO__ of the nominalized verb. The PRED elaborates it (makes it explicit).

CS-3: The reference of the SUBJ is the whole state of affairs expressed by it, construed as a thing. The PRED is an __adverbial__ commentary to this state of affairs.

As can be appreciated, the characteristic of a CS construction is that its SUBJ is a nominalized sentence, and that the predication is constructed as PRED + COP. Types 1 and 2 might be grouped together in that their PRED elaborate an explicit or implicit participant in the SUBJ sentence, while the PRED in type 3 supplies entirely new and unknown information, not already signalled in the SUBJ sentence. But there are differences which call for making the distinction between 1 and 2: In type 1 the nominalized verb may be transitive or intransitive, while in type 2 the verb has to be transitive. In this respect type 3 ‘does not care’.

I will give examples of these types and show how the copula behaves with each of them.

__The type CS-1__

(184)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>[gála&nbsp;</td>
<td>rooré&nbsp;</td>
<td>yánna&nbsp;</td>
<td>horoons</td>
<td>-iɗ</td>
<td>-ɗannó</td>
<td>-<strong>ti</strong>]&nbsp;</td>
<td>daddal</td>
<td>-ááno</td>
<td>-<strong>te</strong></td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>camels&nbsp;</td>
<td>most&nbsp;</td>
<td>time&nbsp;</td>
<td>use</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">ab</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3t</span>.<strong><span style="font-variant: small-caps">nmr</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">t</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nom</span></strong>&nbsp;</td>
<td>trade</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">ag</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pl</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">cop</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">t</span></td>
<td></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘<b>Those who</b> most of the time make use of camels <b>are</b> the traders.’</p>

Agreement patterns in Sidaama may be confusing. Although there is a plural nominalizer (_-ri_), the T-class one (_-ti_) serves well here in view of the coming predicative, which is semantically a plural concept marked as such, but lexically T-class. Agreement may be based on lexical class or on semantics. Besides, all the verbal paradigms equate 3T<span>.</span>SG and 3PL, so when semantics intermingle with lexical class, it is not always possible to decide which of the two possibilities the actual verb form has. Further, there is no COP used only for plural; other factors govern the choice of copula. So very frequently plural meaning is connected with T-class form. On this background one can claim that there is T-class/plural agreement through the last two words in (155).

On the interpretation of this CS it can be said that the readers knew about the camels, the bracketed SUBJ now narrows the topic to the people who use them, refers to them with this nominalization, and makes their identity explicit with the PRED.

The CS construction is very frequently used in questions, where it makes particularly good sense to construe the representative of the wanted information, the factor so ‘new’ that it is unknown, as PRED.

(185)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>[ʔugáát’e&nbsp;</td>
<td>haɗ</td>
<td>-ɗú</td>
<td>-<strong>ri</strong>]&nbsp;</td>
<td>hiikku</td>
<td>-rí</td>
<td>-í<strong>ti</strong>?</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>Hunting&nbsp;</td>
<td>go</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pfct</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3t</span></td>
<td>-<strong><span style="font-variant: small-caps">nmr</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pl</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nom</span></strong>&nbsp;</td>
<td>which</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nmr</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pl</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nom</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">cop</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘Those who went hunting are which ones?’ (=Which are the ones who went hunting?)</p>

Here a similarly conjugated plural verb form as in (155) is nominalized with _-ri_, which always stands for plural. As for the choice of COP, the plural nominalizer (_-ri/-re_) always has the COP _-ti_ (with lengthening of the previous vowel) when its function is to equate two nominal entities, or describe one with an adjective. Here, too, the reference of the bracketed sentence is real world equivalent of the SUBJ of the verb, those who went hunting, and the PRED+COP it used to elicit their identity. With this particular question word, the nominative is used despite the general pattern in Sidaama that PREDs are in the accusative, which with this word would have been ___hiikko__-re-eti._ Compare the next example:

(186)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>ʔánga</td>
<td>-te&nbsp;</td>
<td>koááse&nbsp;</td>
<td>godoʔl</td>
<td>-annó</td>
<td>-<strong>hu</strong>&nbsp;</td>
<td>ʔaé</td>
<td>-é<strong>ti</strong>?</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>hand</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">gen</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">u</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>ball.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>play</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3k</span></td>
<td>-<strong><span style="font-variant: small-caps">nmr</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">k</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nom</span></strong>&nbsp;</td>
<td>who</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">cop</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘He who plays handball is who?’ (Who is the one who is playing handball?)</p>

In both (156) and (157) the CS question tries to elicit the SUBJ of the nominalized verb. In the first one, the PRED is in the nominative, in the second it is in the accusative. There is hardly a semantic difference, so the reason for this discrepancy is unknown. It is suspected that accusative would have been correct in (156) too.

(187)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>[…rooré&nbsp;</td>
<td>ballíʃʃ</td>
<td>-u&nbsp;</td>
<td>k’áál</td>
<td>-la&nbsp;</td>
<td>horoons</td>
<td>-iɗ</td>

<tr class="odd">
<td>most&nbsp;</td>
<td>duplicate&nbsp;(euphemistic)</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">gen</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">u</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>word</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pl</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>use</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">ab</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">3k</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nmr</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">k</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nom</span>&nbsp;</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>méénto</td>
<td>-<strong>ho</strong></td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>women</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">cop</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">k</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘‘He’ who uses the duplicate vocabulary the most, is the women.’ (=They who use euphemisms the most, are the women)</p>

The verb, the nominalizer, and the copula are all lexically K-class, and so is the agreement between them, all governed by the K-class of _meento_. Semantically everything is plural and feminine. (From this it should be obvious why I do not like _masculine_ and _feminine_ as labels of the two nominal classes!) The literal English translation is intended to bring out the seeming anomalousness of the sentence, which however is perfect in Sidaama. The bracketed nominalized sentence refers to the users of a certain vocabulary to replace taboo words, and the PRED identifies them as the women.

Finally a more complicated example of this type:

(188)

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>[[‘kalak’</td>
<td>-am<a href="#ref31" id="31" data-toggle="tooltip" title="The word represents a frequent type of nominalization, viz. INF = N. The addition of INF -a to the derived PAS verb stem kalak’-am- forms both the verbal ‘to create’ (T) and the abstract noun ‘creation, nature’ (K). This is an occurrence of the latter, inflected in GEN.U."><sup>31</sup></a></td>
<td>-ú&nbsp;</td>
<td>jíro</td>
<td>-oti’]&nbsp;</td>
<td>yi</td>
<td>-noonní</td>
<td>-<strong>ri</strong>]&nbsp;</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>create</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pas</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">gen</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">u</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>wealth.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">cop</span>&nbsp;</td>
<td>say</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">us</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pst</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">impf</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nmr</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pl</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nom</span>&nbsp;</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<table style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">
<thead>
<tr class="header" style="font-style:italic;">
<td>hííkko</td>
<td>-ré</td>
<td>-é<strong>ti</strong>?</td>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td>which</td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">nmr</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">pl</span>.<span style="font-variant: small-caps">acc</span></td>
<td>-<span style="font-variant: small-caps">cop</span></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘The things which are called natural resources, which ones are they?’ (Lit.: ‘It is a natural resource’ those things which one has said, are which ones?)</p>

This question has the same structure as the two previous questions, but what is the reference of the nominalized sentence? _-ri_ just tells that ‘here ends the nominalization which is SUBJ, it refers to an entity in PL’. The verb form _yinoonni_ never has a SUBJ; it is the so called impersonal passive or the conjugation of unspecified SUBJ. But it does have a DO, in this case the quote ‘It is a natural resource’, itself a complete sentence. The wanted information here is the entity of which _kalak’amu jirooti_ is a predication, i.e. the covert SUBJ of the quoted sentence. So the reference of the nominalized sentence is not the SUBJ of the highest level verb (it cannot have one), but the SUBJ of a sentence which is quoted, which functions as DO for the highest level verb, and where the SUBJ is only implied. The PRED will encourage the readers to name the natural resources implied, but omitted in that quote.

In the same way as other sentences may be nominalized and in other ways subordinated, CSs may be construed as subordinate too:

(189)

<td>k’oʔlanté&nbsp;</td>
</tr>

<p style="color: #3a87ad; margin-left:30px">‘Because the entity that had a such one (=a long beak) was the (sacred) ibis….’</p>

As an independent sentence the sentence in the outer brackets would have been:

<td>see</td>
